<a id="threat-hypotheses-requiring-validation"></a>
#### 6.2.1 Threat Hypotheses Requiring Validation

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - architecture-derived control gaps not yet source-to-sink proven; treat as leads requiring a validate-or-refute pentest probe before promotion to a finding.

_Architecture- and control-derived threats. Plausible but not yet source-to-sink proven; each entry needs a `validate-or-refute` pentest probe before it becomes a finding._

| ID | Hypothesis | Control Gap | Evidence | Validation |
|-------|----------------------|----------------------|--------|----------------------|
| HYP-001 | XSS exposure from weak output encoding | Template Autoescape, Output Encoding,<br/>Content Security Policy | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |
| HYP-002 | SQL injection exposure from ad-hoc SQL<br/>construction | Parameterized Queries, ORM / Repository<br/>Layer | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |
| HYP-003 | Broken Access Control exposure from<br/>inconsistent AuthZ | Centralised AuthZ Policy, Role / Scope<br/>Enforcement, Ownership Check | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |
| HYP-004 | State-changing route or GraphQL operation<br/>without an authentication gate | Route Authentication Middleware, Server-Side<br/>Session Enforcement | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |
| HYP-005 | Authenticated object-addressing route or<br/>GraphQL operation without an authorization<br/>gate (BOLA/IDOR surface) | Object-Level Ownership Check, Tenant Scoping | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |
| HYP-006 | Broken input validation exposure | Schema Validation, Allowlist Validation | _?_ | _pending validation objective_ |

<a id="password-based-authentication"></a>

**Security assessment**

_Not assessed in detail; see the control overview in [§6.1](#61-security-control-overview)._

**Relevant findings**

- None identified for this control.
#### 6.2.2 Password-Based Authentication

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the login query is built by string interpolation, making the credential check SQL-injectable, and no per-attempt rate limit is applied to the login endpoint.

Password-based authentication is the primary sign-in method for all user accounts. `routes/login.ts` handles `POST /rest/user/login`, verifying the supplied email and password against the Users table and issuing a session JWT on success.

The diagram shows the positive password-login path through the login endpoint:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant FE as Angular SPA
    participant API as POST /rest/user/login
    participant DB as SQLite Users table
    participant JWT as JWT Issuer (lib/insecurity.ts)

    User->>FE: Enter email and password
    FE->>API: POST {email, password}
    API->>DB: SELECT WHERE email=? AND password=MD5(?)
    DB-->>API: User row
    API->>JWT: authorize({id, role, ...})
    JWT-->>API: RS256 signed token
    API-->>FE: {authentication: {token, bid}}
    FE->>FE: localStorage.setItem('token', token)
```

**Security assessment**

The login query at `routes/login.ts:34` builds the WHERE clause by template-literal interpolation - `SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email}'` - making it trivially injectable. The MD5 hash of the password is also interpolated directly into the same string. No rate limit protects `POST /rest/user/login`: the `express-rate-limit` middleware is mounted only at `/rest/user/reset-password` (`server.ts:343`), leaving the login endpoint open to brute force.

- SQL interpolation at `routes/login.ts:34` allows authentication bypass via `' OR '1'='1`.
- No per-IP or per-account brute-force counter on the login path (🟠 [F-014](#f-014)).
- Password storage weakness (MD5, no salt) is assessed in [§6.9.1 Password Hashing](#password-hashing).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-001](#f-001) — Raw SQL string interpolation in the login query enables authentication bypass.
- 🟠 [F-014](#f-014) — No rate-limiting middleware on `POST /rest/user/login` permits unlimited credential-guessing.

**Rate-limiting gap.** `express-rate-limit` is mounted on `/rest/user/reset-password` (`server.ts:343`) and the three `/rest/2fa/*` routes, but not on `POST /rest/user/login`. The existing limiter on the reset path is also bypassable: its `keyGenerator` returns `headers['X-Forwarded-For'] ?? ip`, so an attacker who rotates this header on each request lands in a fresh rate-limit bucket every time (`server.ts:346`). Relevant findings: 🟠 [F-014](#f-014) — No rate limit on `POST /rest/user/login`; 🟠 [F-049](#f-049) — Rate-limit key derived from attacker-controlled `X-Forwarded-For` header enables bypass of existing limiters.

<a id="multi-factor-authentication"></a>
#### 6.2.3 Multi-Factor Authentication

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - TOTP enrollment and verification are implemented but use the same leaked RSA key material as the full session token, undermining the second factor's independence.

TOTP-based 2FA is available as an optional second factor for any account. On login with an MFA-enrolled account, the server issues a short-lived `tmpToken` to gate the TOTP verification step before issuing a full session JWT.

**Security assessment**

The TOTP implementation is structurally sound - enrollment generates a per-user TOTP secret, and the verification route at `/rest/2fa/verify` checks the TOTP code before upgrading to a full session token. The weakness is key reuse: `login.ts:41` calls `security.authorize({userId, type:'password_valid_needs_second_factor_token'})`, which signs the `tmpToken` with the same RSA private key literal at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` that signs full session tokens. Because that key is committed to the public repository (🔴 [F-007](#f-007)), an attacker can mint a valid `tmpToken` directly and skip the TOTP step. Additionally, MFA enrollment is optional and not enforced for administrator accounts, leaving the highest-privilege role unprotected.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-015](#f-015) — The TOTP bypass token is signed with the same public RSA key used for full session tokens, reducing 2FA to a cosmetic gate.
- 🔴 [F-007](#f-007) — Hardcoded RSA private key in source enables arbitrary token forgery, including tmpToken.

<a id="oauth-federated-login"></a>
#### 6.2.4 OAuth / Federated Login

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the OAuth flow derives a deterministic local password from the email claim, and reads the access token directly from the URL fragment with no integrity check.

The OAuth flow runs entirely in the Angular frontend as an implicit-flow adapter. `oauth.component.ts` reads the access token from the redirect URL fragment, calls the Google userinfo endpoint, then derives a local account password and calls the same `POST /rest/user/login` endpoint used for password login.

The diagram shows how the frontend OAuth adapter feeds into the local login flow:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as oauth.component.ts
    participant Google as Google UserInfo API
    participant API as POST /api/Users + /rest/user/login
    participant JWT as JWT Issuer

    User->>SPA: Return from OAuth redirect with token in URL fragment
    SPA->>SPA: parseRedirectUrlParams() reads window.location.href fragment
    SPA->>Google: GET userinfo with access_token
    Google-->>SPA: {email, ...}
    SPA->>SPA: password = btoa(email.split('').reverse().join(''))
    SPA->>API: POST /api/Users (create local account if absent)
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login {email, derived password}
    API->>JWT: authorize(user)
    JWT-->>SPA: RS256 signed session token
```

**Security assessment**

`oauth.component.ts` derives the local password deterministically from the email address (`btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))`) and stores it in plain localStorage. Anyone who knows a target user's email can compute the password and log in without OAuth. The access token is read from `window.location.href` at `app.routing.ts:257` and `oauth.component.ts` with no origin or integrity verification, so a URL-fragment manipulation attack can inject a forged token. The flow also uses the OAuth Implicit grant (token in fragment), which RFC 9700 deprecates in favour of Authorization Code + PKCE.

The derived-password pattern in `oauth.component.ts`:

```ts
const password = btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-009](#f-009) — Deterministic password derived from email claim; any knowledge of the email enables account access without OAuth.
- 🟠 [F-019](#f-019) — Access token read from the raw URL fragment with no integrity or origin check.
- 🟠 [F-020](#f-020) — OAuth Implicit flow puts the bearer token in the URL fragment, exposing it via Referer headers and browser history.

### 6.3 Session and Token Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.3.1 Session Token Signing](#session-token-signing)
- [7.3.2 Session Token Validation](#session-token-validation)
- [7.3.3 Session Token Storage](#session-token-storage)
- [7.3.4 Session Token Revocation](#session-token-revocation)

**Implemented controls:** RS256-signed JWT issuance via `lib/insecurity.ts:54`, token extraction middleware `jwtFrom()` in `lib/utils.ts`, 6-hour fixed expiry on all issued tokens, in-memory `authenticatedUsers` map tracking live sessions.

**Assessment:** This application uses a single locally-signed token format (commonly called JWT) for every authenticated session, regardless of the login flow in [§6.2](#62-identity-and-authentication-controls) that established it. The sub-sections below trace one token through its lifecycle: signing on issuance, validation on every protected request, storage in the browser, manual revocation, and time-based expiry. The signing key is hardcoded in source, the verification step accepts any algorithm the caller nominates, the token is stored in JavaScript-accessible `localStorage`, and no revocation list exists - every stage of the lifecycle has a material weakness.

<a id="session-token-signing"></a><a id="session-token-signing-jwt-based"></a>
#### 6.3.1 Session Token Signing

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the RSA private key used to sign every token is a literal string committed to the public repository, giving any reader the ability to mint arbitrary tokens.

⚠ **Anti-pattern:** Secrets hardcoded in source

`lib/insecurity.ts` exports `authorize()` which signs session JWTs with RS256. The private key material is a string literal embedded at `lib/insecurity.ts:21`. Tokens are issued on every successful login, TOTP bypass, and OAuth flow termination.

The diagram shows the signing flow from login through to the bearer token returned to the client:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant Login as routes/login.ts
    participant Insec as lib/insecurity.ts
    participant FE as Angular SPA

    User->>Login: POST /rest/user/login
    Login->>Insec: security.authorize({id, role, email})
    Insec->>Insec: jwt.sign(payload, privateKey, {algorithm:'RS256', expiresIn:'6h'})
    Insec-->>Login: signed JWT
    Login-->>FE: {authentication: {token, bid}}
    FE->>FE: localStorage.setItem('token', token)
```

**Security assessment**

The RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` is committed to the public OWASP Juice Shop repository. Any reader of the source can call `jwt.sign(payload, privateKey, {algorithm:'RS256'})` to produce a token the server accepts as `role=admin`. The HMAC secret for coupon tokens at `lib/insecurity.ts:42` (`'pa4qacea4VK9t9nGv7yZtwmj'`) is similarly hardcoded and allows coupon-code forgery. The deluxe-membership token (`insecurity.ts:150`) also derives from the same leaked key material.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007](#f-007) — RSA private key literal in `lib/insecurity.ts:21` enables arbitrary JWT forgery.
- 🔴 [F-017](#f-017) — Hardcoded HMAC secret for coupon tokens allows coupon forgery without authentication.
- 🔴 [F-057](#f-057) — Deluxe-membership token HMAC reuses the leaked private key.

<a id="session-token-validation"></a><a id="session-token-validation-jwt-based"></a>
#### 6.3.2 Session Token Validation

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the verification function accepts the algorithm nominated by the caller rather than enforcing RS256, enabling algorithm-confusion token forgery.

`verify()` at `lib/insecurity.ts:55` checks the signature on incoming bearer tokens. Protected routes call this function or the `security.authenticatedUsers.get(token)` cache. The frontend's `request.interceptor.ts` attaches the stored token as the `Authorization: Bearer` header on every API call.

The diagram shows the validation path on a protected request:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor Client
    participant MW as Token Middleware
    participant Insec as lib/insecurity.ts verify()
    participant Route as Protected Route

    Client->>MW: GET /api/... + Authorization: Bearer token
    MW->>Insec: security.verify(token)
    Insec->>Insec: jws.verify(token, publicKey) - no alg whitelist
    Insec-->>MW: true / false
    alt Current state
        MW->>Route: passes through if verify() returns truthy
        Route-->>Client: 200 OK with data
    else After mitigation
        MW->>Insec: jwt.verify(token, publicKey, {algorithms:['RS256']})
        Insec-->>MW: throws if alg !== RS256
        MW-->>Client: 401 Unauthorized
    end
```

**Security assessment**

`verify()` calls `jws.verify(token, publicKey)` with no algorithm restriction argument. `jws.verify` honours the `alg` header in the token itself: a token with `"alg":"none"` passes verification with an empty signature string, and a token with `"alg":"HS256"` would be verified using the public key as the HMAC secret. Role-check helpers `isAccounting()`, `isDeluxe()`, and `isCustomer()` in `lib/insecurity.ts:156–171` call `decode()` after `verify()` but use the decoded claims from `jwtFrom(req)` - meaning a token that passes the broken `verify()` gates the role check on unvalidated claims (🔴 [F-050](#f-050)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-008](#f-008) — `jws.verify()` with no algorithm whitelist accepts `alg:none` tokens.
- 🔴 [F-050](#f-050) — Role-check helpers read unverified decoded claims after the broken `verify()` call.

<a id="session-token-storage"></a><a id="session-token-storage-browser-localstorage"></a>
#### 6.3.3 Session Token Storage

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the session JWT is persisted in `localStorage`, readable by any JavaScript executing on the origin, and the OAuth flow also writes the token to a JavaScript-set cookie that cannot carry `HttpOnly`.

⚠ **Anti-pattern:** SPA without BFF

`app.guard.ts:18` reads `localStorage.getItem('token')` to check login state. `oauth.component.ts:51` writes the token to `localStorage` after login. `request.interceptor.ts:13` reads the same value and injects it as the `Authorization` header on every outgoing API request.

**Security assessment**

`localStorage` is accessible to any script running in the page's origin - an XSS payload that exfiltrates `localStorage.getItem('token')` captures the full session JWT with no additional privilege. The Angular SPA stores credentials in the browser with no Backend-for-Frontend to hold them server-side in an `HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Strict` cookie. `oauth.component.ts:50` additionally writes the token via the `ngy-cookie` client-side library, which cannot set `HttpOnly` from JavaScript, producing a cookie readable by XSS as well (🟠 [F-047](#f-047)). Moving token storage server-side to an `HttpOnly` cookie (BFF pattern) would eliminate the XSS-token-theft vector entirely.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-046](#f-046) — JWT stored in `localStorage`, readable by XSS.
- 🟠 [F-047](#f-047) — Session token also written to a non-`HttpOnly` cookie via the JavaScript cookie library.

<a id="session-token-revocation"></a>
#### 6.3.4 Session Token Revocation

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no revocation list exists; an issued token remains valid until its 6-hour expiry regardless of logout, password change, or account compromise.

`authorize()` in `lib/insecurity.ts:54` issues stateless RS256 tokens with a fixed 6-hour expiry (`expiresIn: '6h'`). The `authenticatedUsers` in-memory map (line 70) stores live sessions but is not consulted as a revocation check - `verify()` only validates the signature.

**Security assessment**

Logging out from the Angular SPA removes the token from `localStorage`, but the token itself remains cryptographically valid until expiry. If a token is stolen via XSS or exfiltrated, the server has no mechanism to invalidate it before the 6-hour window closes. A server-side token denylist keyed on `jti` would close this gap; alternatively, shortening the expiry to 15 minutes and issuing a short-lived refresh token via `HttpOnly` cookie would bound exposure without breaking user experience.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-054](#f-054) — Stateless token lifecycle with no revocation capability; stolen tokens are valid for up to 6 hours.

The diagram below shows the full token lifecycle from issuance through browser storage to validation, making the attack surface visible at a glance:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular SPA (localStorage)
    participant API as Express Backend
    participant Verify as security.verify()

    User->>API: POST /rest/user/login
    API-->>SPA: JWT (RS256, 6h expiry)
    SPA->>SPA: localStorage.setItem('token', jwt)
    User->>SPA: Navigate to protected page
    SPA->>API: GET /api/... Authorization: Bearer jwt
    API->>Verify: jws.verify(jwt, publicKey) - no alg check
    alt Current state — alg:none bypass
        Verify-->>API: true (no signature required)
        API-->>SPA: 200 OK - data returned
    else After mitigation — algorithm whitelist
        Verify-->>API: throws if alg !== RS256
        API-->>SPA: 401 Unauthorized
    end
```

### 6.4 Authorization Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.4.1 Role-Based Access Control](#role-based-access-control)
- [7.4.2 Object-Level Authorization](#object-level-authorization)
- [7.4.3 Management Endpoint Protection](#management-endpoint-protection)

**Implemented controls:** Server-side role checks via `security.authenticatedUsers.get(token)` in sensitive routes, Angular guards (`AdminGuard`, `DeluxeGuard`, `AccountingGuard`) for UI-layer gating, Sequelize `findOne`/`findAll` with `UserId` filtering on basket and order routes, admin-only route middleware in `/api/Admin/**` handlers.

**Assessment:** A two-layer enforcement model exists on paper: frontend Angular guards check the decoded JWT role before activating a route, and backend route handlers re-verify the role server-side. The gap is that several routes rely on attacker-controllable values in the request body (`req.body.UserId`) rather than the authenticated session to determine ownership (🔴 [F-010](#f-010)), and the client-side guards remain the only access check on some management surfaces (🟠 [F-006](#f-006)). Mass assignment via the Sequelize auto-generated REST endpoint allows any registration request to set privileged fields like `role` (🔴 [F-002](#f-002)).

<a id="role-based-access-control"></a>
#### 6.4.1 Role-Based Access Control

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - server-side role checks exist but are gated on the broken `verify()` result, and the Angular client-side guards remain the sole enforcement on some routes.

Role-based access control assigns each JWT a `role` claim (`admin`, `customer`, `deluxe`, `accounting`) and enforces access at both the Angular route level and in individual Express route handlers. The backend resolves the role by looking up the token in the `authenticatedUsers` map (`lib/insecurity.ts:70`).

**Security assessment**

Server-side role enforcement depends on `security.verify(token)` passing - which it does for `alg:none` tokens as described in [§6.3.2](#session-token-validation). Route helpers `isAccounting()`, `isDeluxe()`, `isCustomer()` in `lib/insecurity.ts:156–171` derive role from `decode(jwtFrom(req)).data.role`, not from a server-validated session object. The `AdminGuard` at `frontend/src/app/app.guard.ts:53` decodes the token client-side with `jwtDecode` and grants admin access if the payload matches - this guard can be bypassed by disabling JavaScript or modifying the decoded value in browser memory.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-006](#f-006) — `AdminGuard` and peer guards perform all role enforcement client-side; server-side re-verification is absent on several routes.
- 🔴 [F-050](#f-050) — Role decisions based on decoded claims from the broken `verify()` path allow a forged token to escalate to any role.
- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — State-changing routes mounted in `server.ts` without an authentication or authorization gate.

<a id="object-level-authorization"></a><a id="object-level-authorization-ownership-checks"></a>
#### 6.4.2 Object-Level Authorization

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - ownership checks exist on basket and order routes but trust `req.body.UserId` supplied by the caller rather than the authenticated session identity.

Several resource routes - `routes/basketItems.ts`, `routes/userProfile.ts`, `routes/deluxe.ts` - filter data by `UserId` before returning results. The check is typically a Sequelize `findOne({where: {UserId: req.body.UserId}})` or equivalent.

**Security assessment**

Trusting `req.body.UserId` for ownership means any authenticated user can read or modify another user's resources by supplying a different ID in the request body. The server never derives the ownership key from the verified session (`req.auth.id` or equivalent from the JWT claim post-verification) - the attacker controls the filter value. This class of vulnerability affects basket items, wallet operations, payment cards, and user-profile updates (🔴 [F-010](#f-010)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-010](#f-010) — Object-level authorization uses attacker-controlled `req.body.UserId` rather than the authenticated session identity.
- 🔴 [F-002](#f-002) — Mass assignment allows a registration payload to set `role` and other privileged model fields.

<a id="management-endpoint-protection"></a>
#### 6.4.3 Management Endpoint Protection

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - admin and management routes carry server-side role checks, but several sensitive operational surfaces are exposed without authentication.

Admin functionality under `/api/Admin/**` is guarded by role verification. The `Swagger UI` documentation endpoint (`/api-docs/`) and the Prometheus metrics endpoint (`GET /metrics`) are mounted without authentication middleware.

**Security assessment**

`/api-docs/` is reachable without any credential and exposes the full API contract including request schemas for all endpoints (`server.ts` mounts swagger-ui-express with no auth guard). The Prometheus metrics endpoint at `server.ts:676,729` returns user registration counts by customer type, file-upload counters, and LLM token counters - all without authentication (🟡 [F-062](#f-062)). The FTP simulation route (`/ftp/`) is mounted with `serve-index` and no authentication at `server.ts:269-270`, exposing a browsable file listing (🟠 [F-037](#f-037)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — Sensitive routes registered without authorization middleware in `server.ts`.
- 🟡 [F-062](#f-062) — Prometheus metrics endpoint at `/metrics` unauthenticated, leaking operational counters.
- 🟠 [F-037](#f-037) — Public FTP directory listing without authentication exposes files including backups.

### 6.5 Query Construction and Data Access Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.5.1 Parameterized Query / ORM Usage](#parameterized-query-orm-usage)

**Implemented controls:** Sequelize ORM with parameterized queries on most resource routes (`findOne`, `findAll`, `create` with bound values), MarsDB abstraction for MongoDB-style reviews.

**Assessment:** Sequelize ORM covers the majority of data-access paths, and most routes use bound parameters. The critical exceptions are the authentication query in `routes/login.ts:34` and the product-search query, both of which bypass the ORM and call `models.sequelize.query()` with a raw string that embeds `req.body.email` and the search term directly. The NoSQL review lookup in `routes/productReviews.ts` passes attacker-controlled data into a MongoDB `$where` JavaScript expression.

<a id="parameterized-query-orm-usage"></a>
#### 6.5.1 Parameterized Query / ORM Usage

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the login and product-search routes bypass Sequelize and concatenate user input directly into raw SQL strings; the review route uses a MongoDB `$where` operator with user-controlled input.

Sequelize manages most relational access, and the ORM's query builder correctly parameterizes values for `findOne`, `findAll`, and `create` calls. `models/index.ts` instantiates a single SQLite connection shared across all routes. MongoDB-style review queries use MarsDB.

The vulnerable login query that bypasses the ORM:

```ts
models.sequelize.query(
  `SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password)}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`
)
```

**Security assessment**

Two raw SQL call sites concatenate request values without binding:

- `routes/login.ts:34` - the authentication query embeds `req.body.email` and the MD5-hashed password; a payload of `' OR '1'='1` in the email field bypasses authentication and returns the first user row (typically the seeded admin account).
- The product-search route embeds the search term in a `LIKE '%${criteria}%'` clause via the same `models.sequelize.query` path.

The `$where` operator in the MarsDB review lookup (`routes/productReviews.ts`) executes server-side JavaScript, and while `productId` is `Number()`-coerced at that call site, the same `$where` pattern in adjacent code accepts string input unchecked (🔴 [F-027](#f-027) (NoSQL/JS injection in MongoDB \$where review lookup)). The in-process SQLite database gives a successful SQL injection the full blast radius of the application's DB principal - no row-level security, no separate read/write accounts (🟠 [F-053](#f-053)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-001](#f-001) — Raw SQL interpolation in the login and search routes enables authentication bypass and data exfiltration.
- 🔴 [F-027](#f-027) — MongoDB `$where` operator in the review lookup executes server-side JavaScript with attacker-controlled operands.
- 🟠 [F-053](#f-053) — In-process SQLite with a single DB principal amplifies SQL injection blast radius.

### 6.6 Input Boundary Validation Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.6.1 Validation Approach](#validation-approach)
- [7.6.2 File Upload & Deserialization Constraints](#file-upload-deserialization-constraints)

**Implemented controls:** `multer` file-size limit on multipart uploads, `express-rate-limit` on the reset-password path, Sequelize model validators on numeric fields (`e.g`. card number range), Angular reactive-form validators on the frontend.

**Assessment:** Frontend form validators are present but provide no security guarantee since they are client-side only. The backend applies no schema validation middleware (no `express-validator`, `zod`, or JSON Schema enforcement) on request bodies before routes consume them. File upload validation checks the MIME type and size via multer but not the file content or extension against a strict allowlist.

<a id="validation-approach"></a>
#### 6.6.1 Validation Approach

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - no server-side schema validation exists on request bodies; routes consume `req.body.*` fields directly with only ad-hoc per-field checks.

Angular reactive forms provide validation feedback in the UI. On the backend, Express routes access `req.body.<field>` without passing through a shared validation layer.

**Security assessment**

Widespread direct consumption of `req.body.UserId`, `req.body.email`, `req.body.quantity`, and similar fields with no schema enforcement allows parameter tampering across order, basket, wallet, and profile routes. The absence of a centralized validation layer means each route must independently guard its own inputs - and several do not. `req.body.imageUrl` at `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` is passed directly to `fetch(url)` with no scheme or host allowlist, enabling SSRF (🟠 [F-045](#f-045)). The open-redirect handler at `lib/insecurity.ts:136` validates using `url.includes(allowedUrl)` - a substring match rather than an origin check - allowing a URL like `http://attacker.example/phish#https://github.com/juice-shop/juice-shop` to pass (🟠 [F-022](#f-022)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-004](#f-004) — Unbounded input to the B2B order evaluator with no size or content allowlist.
- 🟠 [F-022](#f-022) — Open-redirect allowlist uses substring matching rather than exact origin comparison.
- 🟠 [F-045](#f-045) — `imageUrl` parameter passed directly to `fetch()` with no SSRF guard.

<a id="file-upload-deserialization-constraints"></a>
#### 6.6.2 File Upload & Deserialization Constraints

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the archive extractor writes entry paths from attacker-controlled archive members without path-containment checks, and the XML parser has DTD loading and external-entity resolution enabled.

`routes/fileUpload.ts` handles multipart uploads via `multer` and dispatches the file to type-specific handlers: XML files go to `handleXmlUpload`, ZIP files to `extractZipBuffer`, and other files are processed with YAML evaluation in a `Node\.js` VM sandbox.

**Security assessment**

Three distinct deserialization weaknesses coexist in the single upload route:

- **Zip Slip** (`routes/fileUpload.ts:30–34`): `extractZipBuffer` iterates archive entries and writes each one as `'uploads/complaints/' + fileName` where `fileName` is the attacker-controlled `entry.path`. A crafted entry path such as `../../lib/insecurity.ts` traverses outside the target directory.
- **XXE** (`lib/xml.ts:35`): `parseXmlString` is configured with `XML_PARSE_NOENT | XML_PARSE_DTDLOAD`, which resolves external entity references. A DOCTYPE that references `file:///etc/passwd` will be included in the parsed output.
- **Sandbox eval** (`routes/fileUpload.ts`, `routes/b2bOrder.ts:23`): YAML content is evaluated in a `vm.runInContext` sandbox using the `notevil` interpreter; `notevil ^1.3.3` does not block `__proto__` / `constructor.prototype` writes, enabling prototype pollution leading to sandbox escape (🟠 [F-024](#f-024)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-033](#f-033) — Zip Slip path traversal in the archive extractor at `routes/fileUpload.ts:34`.
- 🔴 [F-012](#f-012) — XXE via external entity resolution in `lib/xml.ts:35` with DTD loading enabled.
- 🔴 [F-013](#f-013) — Server-side code evaluation of `orderLinesData` via the `notevil` sandbox at `routes/b2bOrder.ts:23`.
- 🟠 [F-024](#f-024) — Prototype pollution through the `notevil` sandbox escape enables object shape manipulation.

### 6.7 Output Encoding and Rendering Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.7.1 Template Sanitization](#template-sanitization)

**Implemented controls:** Angular's default template escaping applies to interpolation (`{{ }}`) and standard attribute bindings throughout the SPA, blocking reflected HTML injection in standard rendering paths.

**Assessment:** Angular's default escaping provides a baseline, but two explicit sanitizer-bypass call sites in the frontend suppress it deliberately. No Content-Security-Policy header is served with the SPA document to limit the impact of XSS exploitation.

<a id="template-sanitization"></a><a id="template-sanitization-angular"></a>
#### 6.7.1 Template Sanitization

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Angular's automatic escaping is active for standard template bindings, but two components call `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` to inject raw HTML into the DOM.

Angular's `DomSanitizer` runs on all template interpolations by default, encoding HTML-special characters before DOM insertion. The bypass API `sanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` is available for cases where trusted HTML must be rendered.

The bypass that injects user-controlled search output into the DOM:

```ts
// search-result.component.ts:143
this.searchValue = this.sanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml(queryParam)
```

**Security assessment**

`search-result.component.ts:143` binds the URL `q` parameter directly into the DOM via `bypassSecurityTrustHtml(queryParam)`, creating a reflected XSS sink. `data-export.component.ts:71` calls `window.open('', '_blank').document.write(this.userData)`, injecting user-controlled export data into a new window's document - a persistent XSS path when the stored data contains script. Helmet's XSS filter is explicitly commented out in the startup configuration with a note reading "no protection from persisted XSS via RESTful API" (🔴 [F-025](#f-025)), and no CSP limits what scripts can execute in the page context (🟡 [F-060](#f-060)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-035](#f-035) — `bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` on the search query parameter creates a reflected XSS sink.
- 🔴 [F-025](#f-025) — Helmet `xssFilter` explicitly disabled; persistent XSS surface left unmitigated.

### 6.8 Browser and Cross-Origin Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.8.1 CORS Policy](#cors-policy)
- [7.8.2 Content Security Policy](#content-security-policy)
- [7.8.3 CSRF Protection](#csrf-protection)

**Implemented controls:** Helmet middleware with `noSniff()` and `frameguard()` applied; `cors()` npm package mounted globally via `server.ts:182`; the SPA sends `Authorization: Bearer` tokens rather than cookies for most routes, reducing CSRF scope on those paths.

**Assessment:** The CORS configuration reflects any origin with the `cors()` default, the Content-Security-Policy header is absent from the SPA document, and no CSRF token is issued. The combination means a malicious cross-origin page can issue authenticated API requests to any `/api/*` or `/rest/*` endpoint on behalf of a logged-in victim, and any XSS payload can load arbitrary external scripts without policy restriction.

<a id="cors-policy"></a>
#### 6.8.1 CORS Policy

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - `cors()` is mounted with no options, reflecting any incoming `Origin` header and implicitly allowing cross-origin requests with credentials on all routes.

`server.ts:182-183` mounts the `cors` npm package with no argument: `app.use(cors())`. This sets `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` to the reflected request origin for all routes, including the authenticated `/rest/*` and `/api/*` endpoints.

**Security assessment**

With the reflected wildcard, a malicious site visited by an authenticated Juice Shop user can issue cross-origin `fetch()` requests to `/api/Users` or `/rest/user/change-password` and read the responses. Because many state-changing routes accept `Authorization: Bearer <token>` from the request (not a cookie), CORS is the primary cross-origin protection boundary on those paths - and it is completely open. Locking the `origin` option to the production hostname would close this gap.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-038](#f-038) — Wildcard CORS via default `cors()` reflects any origin on all routes.

<a id="content-security-policy"></a>
#### 6.8.2 Content Security Policy

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - Helmet provides `noSniff` and `frameguard` headers, but no Content-Security-Policy is sent with the SPA document, leaving the page's script execution unrestricted.

Helmet is configured in the Express startup and applies several security headers: `X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff` and `X-Frame-Options: SAMEORIGIN`. The `helmet.contentSecurityPolicy()` and `helmet.xssFilter()` calls are commented out.

**Security assessment**

`index.html` ships without a `Content-Security-Policy` meta tag or response header (confirmed at lines 8–29 of the document), and the SPA loads Google Fonts CSS and other third-party resources without Subresource Integrity. With confirmed XSS sinks at `search-result.component.ts:143` and `data-export.component.ts:71`, the absence of a CSP means any script injected via those sinks executes without restriction - including cross-origin data exfiltration and keylogging. A `default-src 'self'` base policy would reduce the blast radius materially.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-060](#f-060) — No Content-Security-Policy on the SPA document; third-party resources loaded without Subresource Integrity.
- 🔴 [F-025](#f-025) — Helmet XSS filter disabled explicitly, compounding the missing CSP.

<a id="csrf-protection"></a>
#### 6.8.3 CSRF Protection

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no CSRF token is issued or validated on any state-changing route.

The SPA sends the session JWT as a `Bearer` token in the `Authorization` header for most routes, which provides implicit CSRF protection on those paths. Cookie-based authentication flows (OAuth path, `req.cookies.token` routes) do not benefit from this protection.

**Security assessment**

Routes that read `req.cookies.token` rather than the `Authorization` header - including `routes/currentUser.ts:12` - are accessible to cross-origin requests that include cookies. Because no `SameSite` attribute is set on the token cookie and no CSRF token is validated, a cross-origin request from a page the user visits can read the current-user response. The open CORS policy (🟠 [F-038](#f-038)) amplifies this: `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` reflects the attacker's origin, permitting the browser to expose the response to the cross-origin script. A `SameSite=Strict` cookie attribute or a synchronizer token pattern on the cookie-authenticated routes would close this gap.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-038](#f-038) — Open CORS combined with cookie-based authentication routes creates a CSRF-equivalent exposure.
- 🟠 [F-047](#f-047) — Session token stored in a JavaScript-set cookie with no `SameSite` or `HttpOnly` attribute.

### 6.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.9.1 Password Hashing](#password-hashing)
- [7.9.2 Secret and Key Management](#secret-and-key-management)
- [7.9.3 Transport Encryption](#transport-encryption)
- [7.9.4 Secure Cookie Flags](#secure-cookie-flags)

**Implemented controls:** RS256 algorithm for JWT signing (stronger than HS256), distroless runtime image (reduces key-extraction surface), environment-specific configuration files in `config/`.

**Assessment:** The RS256 algorithm choice is the sole positive cryptographic control. Everything downstream is defeated: the signing key is hardcoded in source (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`), passwords are stored as unsalted MD5 hashes, HMAC secrets for coupon and security-answer tokens are hardcoded string literals, and payment card numbers are stored in cleartext. Rotating the signing key, adopting bcrypt/Argon2 for passwords, and moving all secrets to environment variables would collectively close the most critical crypto gaps.

<a id="password-hashing"></a>
#### 6.9.1 Password Hashing

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - passwords are stored as unsalted MD5 digests, which are crackable offline in seconds with precomputed rainbow tables.

The `User` model's password setter at `models/user.ts:76` calls `security.hash(password)` before storing the value. `security.hash()` is defined in `lib/insecurity.ts:44` as `crypto.createHash('md5').update(data).digest('hex')`.

The password storage function that applies MD5 without a salt:

```ts
// lib/insecurity.ts:44
const hash = (data: string) => crypto.createHash('md5').update(data).digest('hex')
```

**Security assessment**

MD5 is a message-digest function, not a password-hashing function. It produces a 32-character hex string with no cost factor, no salt, and no memory hardness. A database breach yields credentials that can be reversed against public MD5 reverse-lookup tables instantly. Common passwords (`e.g`. `admin123`) are pre-imaged in every public rainbow table. Adopting `bcrypt` (work factor ≥12) or `argon2id` as the password hasher would bound offline cracking to infeasible time.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-011](#f-011) — Unsalted MD5 password storage in `lib/insecurity.ts:44`; all user credentials are reversible offline.

<a id="secret-and-key-management"></a><a id="secret-management"></a>
#### 6.9.2 Secret and Key Management

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the RSA private key, the HMAC secret, the security-answer HMAC key, and the coupon signing key are all hardcoded string literals in `lib/insecurity.ts`.

⚠ **Anti-pattern:** Secrets hardcoded in source

`lib/insecurity.ts` is the central cryptographic utility module. It contains the RSA private key at line 21 (used to sign every session JWT), the HMAC secret `'pa4qacea4VK9t9nGv7yZtwmj'` at line 42 (used for coupon tokens), and `security.hmac()` which is also called by `models/securityAnswer.ts:45` to hash stored security answers.

**Security assessment**

All key material is committed to the public repository. Any reader can:

- Sign arbitrary JWTs with `role=admin` using the leaked RSA private key (🔴 [F-007](#f-007)).
- Forge coupon codes by computing `HMAC-SHA256('pa4qacea4VK9t9nGv7yZtwmj', couponString)` (🔴 [F-017](#f-017)).
- Look up a user's security-answer hash offline given the hardcoded HMAC key (🔴 [F-067](#f-067)).
- Forge deluxe-membership tokens using the same RSA private key (🔴 [F-057](#f-057)).

Moving all secrets to runtime-injected environment variables (`e.g`. `process.env.JWT_PRIVATE_KEY`) and rotating the keys immediately would end the exposure. The `config/` directory contains environment-specific YAML files but none inject signing key material at runtime.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007](#f-007) — RSA private key hardcoded at `lib/insecurity.ts:21`.
- 🔴 [F-017](#f-017) — Coupon HMAC secret hardcoded at `lib/insecurity.ts:42`.
- 🔴 [F-067](#f-067) — Security-answer HMAC key shared with the coupon key, also hardcoded.
- 🔴 [F-057](#f-057) — Deluxe-token HMAC derives from the same leaked RSA private key.

<a id="transport-encryption"></a><a id="transport-encryption-tls"></a>
#### 6.9.3 Transport Encryption

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - the application listens on HTTP (port 3000) with no TLS termination in the Express layer; TLS is expected to be provided by a reverse proxy or cloud load balancer in production deployments.

The Express server binds to port 3000 over HTTP. The Dockerfile exposes port 3000. No `https.createServer` or TLS certificate configuration is present in `app.ts` or `server.ts`. Production deployments of OWASP Juice Shop are typically fronted by an HTTPS reverse proxy.

**Security assessment**

In the reference Docker deployment, all traffic between the browser and the `Node\.js` process travels over plain HTTP unless a reverse proxy adds TLS. With the JWT stored in `localStorage` and transmitted as a `Bearer` token in the `Authorization` header, interception on an unencrypted link exposes the session token directly. This is a deployment configuration concern rather than a code defect, but the codebase provides no TLS configuration or HSTS header to push operators toward the secure deployment model.

**Relevant findings**

- No dedicated transport-encryption finding is routed here; the exposure is amplified by 🔴 [F-046](#f-046) (JWT in localStorage) and 🟠 [F-047](#f-047) (non-HttpOnly cookie).

<a id="secure-cookie-flags"></a>
#### 6.9.4 Secure Cookie Flags

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - cookies are set from JavaScript without `HttpOnly`, and no `Secure` or `SameSite` attribute is applied to the token cookie.

The OAuth component writes the session token to a cookie via `this.cookieService.put('token', authentication.token, { expires })` at `oauth.component.ts:50`. The `ngy-cookie` library calls `document.cookie` under the hood, which cannot set `HttpOnly`.

**Security assessment**

A cookie set from JavaScript (`document.cookie`) cannot carry the `HttpOnly` flag - making it readable by any script on the origin exactly like `localStorage`. The absence of `Secure` means the cookie may be transmitted over unencrypted HTTP. The absence of `SameSite=Strict` or `SameSite=Lax` means the cookie is included in cross-origin requests, contributing to the CSRF-equivalent risk at the cookie-authenticated routes (🟠 [F-047](#f-047)). Server-side `res.cookie('token', value, {httpOnly:true, secure:true, sameSite:'strict'})` would eliminate both the JavaScript-readability and CSRF dimensions.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-047](#f-047) — Session token cookie written from JavaScript; no `HttpOnly`, `Secure`, or `SameSite` attribute.

### 6.10 File Parser and Outbound Request Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.10.1 Outbound Request (SSRF) Controls](#outbound-request-ssrf-controls)

**Implemented controls:** `multer` file-size limiting on upload endpoints, `serve-index` directory listing scoped to the `/ftp/` path, extension-based MIME dispatch for uploaded files.

**Assessment:** The file-upload endpoint at `/file-upload` dispatches uploaded content to four distinct parsers (XML via `libxmljs2`, ZIP via `unzipper`, YAML/eval via `notevil`, and raw file storage), each of which has a distinct vulnerability. The SSRF path via `profileImageUrlUpload.ts` accepts arbitrary URLs with no allowlist. The `/ftp/` directory listing and `/file-serve/` route are publicly accessible without authentication.

<a id="outbound-request-ssrf-controls"></a>
#### 6.10.1 Outbound Request (SSRF) Controls

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - the profile-image URL upload passes the caller-supplied URL directly to `fetch()` without an allowlist; the `/ftp/` and `/file-serve/` endpoints serve files without authentication.

`routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` calls `fetch(req.body.imageUrl)` and writes the response bytes to `frontend/dist/frontend/assets/public/`. No scheme, host, or IP range check is applied before the fetch. The `serve-index` middleware at `server.ts:269-270` lists the `/ftp/` directory to unauthenticated callers.

**Security assessment**

An authenticated user can supply `imageUrl=http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/` to probe cloud instance metadata, or `imageUrl=http://localhost:3000/metrics` to read the Prometheus metrics endpoint that is also accessible without authentication (🟡 [F-062](#f-062)). An allowlist of permitted image-hosting domains (`e.g`. `gravatar.com`, `example.com`) plus a check that the resolved IP is not in RFC 1918 / link-local ranges would contain the SSRF surface. The FTP directory listing at `/ftp/` exposes backup files, coupon data files, and historical sensitive documents to any user (🟠 [F-037](#f-037)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-045](#f-045) — SSRF via `fetch(req.body.imageUrl)` at `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24`.
- 🟠 [F-037](#f-037) — Public FTP directory listing without authentication.
- 🟠 [F-034](#f-034) — File extension derived from attacker-controlled URL trailing token allows SVG/HTML upload to the public assets directory.

### 6.11 Operations Runtime and Supply Chain Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.11.1 Supply Chain & Dependency Integrity](#supply-chain-dependency-integrity)
- [7.11.2 Container Runtime Hardening](#container-runtime-hardening)
- [7.11.3 Logging & Monitoring](#logging-monitoring)
- [7.11.4 Automated SCA scanning](#automated-sca-scanning)
- [7.11.5 Automated dependency updates](#automated-dependency-updates)
- [7.11.6 Lockfile hygiene](#lockfile-hygiene)

**Implemented controls:** Distroless runtime base image (`gcr.io/distroless/nodejs24-debian13`), non-root user (`USER 65532`) in the runtime stage, GitHub Actions CodeQL scan wired for JavaScript, multi-stage Dockerfile separating build and runtime layers, GitHub Actions CI pipeline running lint and test on every push.

**Assessment:** The distroless runtime image and non-root UID are genuine hardening steps that reduce post-exploitation surface. All other supply chain controls are absent or defeated: the installer stage runs `npm install` without `--ignore-scripts` on PR code, the base image is pinned by tag rather than digest, automated dependency update tooling (Dependabot/Renovate) is absent, and the lockfile mechanism is disabled at the project level via `.npmrc`.

<a id="supply-chain-dependency-integrity"></a>
#### 6.11.1 Supply Chain & Dependency Integrity

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - several CI jobs run `npm install` without `--ignore-scripts`, allowing postinstall build hooks in PR code or malicious packages to execute arbitrary commands during the test pipeline.

The CI workflow in `.github/workflows/ci.yml` runs `npm install` as the first step in six distinct test jobs (server-test, api-test, frontend-test, e2e-test, coding-challenge-rsn, custom-config-test). `package.json` defines a `postinstall` script: `cd frontend && npm install && npm run build:frontend`.

**Security assessment**

`package.json`'s `postinstall` hook runs on every `npm install` call in CI - including when processing a pull request from an external contributor. This means an external contributor's PR can include a malicious `package.json` change that executes arbitrary commands on the GitHub Actions runner during the `npm install` step (🟠 [F-030](#f-030)). Third-party GitHub Actions images pinned to mutable branch tags (`e.g`. `calibreapp/image-actions@main` at `image_actions.yml:33`) will re-resolve to whatever commit the publisher pushes, enabling supply-chain injection at the workflow level (🟠 [F-031](#f-031)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-030](#f-030) — `npm install` without `--ignore-scripts` in CI runs the `postinstall` build hook on untrusted PR code.
- 🟠 [F-031](#f-031) — Third-party Action at `image_actions.yml:33` pinned to mutable `@main` branch tag.
- 🟠 [F-040](#f-040) — GitHub Actions workflows missing a `permissions:` block, granting over-broad `GITHUB_TOKEN` access.

<a id="container-hardening"></a><a id="container-runtime-hardening"></a>
#### 6.11.2 Container Runtime Hardening

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - the runtime stage uses a distroless image and a non-root UID, but the installer stage lacks `--ignore-scripts` and the runtime image is pinned by tag, not by digest.

The Dockerfile uses a multi-stage build: an `installer` stage on `node:24` compiles the application, and the final stage copies only the built output into `gcr.io/distroless/nodejs24-debian13` running as `USER 65532` (nonroot).

**Security assessment**

The distroless base image eliminates the shell, package manager, and most system utilities from the runtime, materially reducing post-exploitation pivot options. The non-root UID `65532` means a container-escape exploit starts without root privileges. The gap is in image reproducibility: `gcr.io/distroless/nodejs24-debian13` is referenced by tag without a digest hash, so the image that a CI run pulls may differ from the image that production runs (🟠 [F-042](#f-042)). The installer stage's `npm install --omit=dev` without `--ignore-scripts` (in the Dockerfile) also runs postinstall hooks during image build.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-042](#f-042) — Dockerfile base image pinned to a mutable tag without a digest hash.
- 🟡 [F-064](#f-064) — Non-root USER directive is present and correct; this is a positive control.
- 🟡 [F-066](#f-066) — `npm install` in the installer stage runs without `--ignore-scripts`.

<a id="logging-monitoring"></a>
#### 6.11.3 Logging & Monitoring

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - `morgan` logs HTTP access lines in the Express middleware stack, but no structured security event log captures authentication events, authorization failures, or token issuance.

`morgan` is mounted in `server.ts` and logs each HTTP request method, path, status code, and response time to stdout. The Prometheus metrics endpoint at `GET /metrics` exposes runtime counters including user registration totals and LLM token usage.

**Security assessment**

Authentication events - successful and failed logins, token issuance, password resets, account creation - are not captured as structured security audit events with actor, action, resource, and timestamp. After an account takeover, the only forensic trail available is the raw HTTP access log, which records the IP and path but not the identity or session context. An attacker who rotates IPs leaves no correlated audit record. The Prometheus endpoint also leaks user metrics publicly (🟡 [F-062](#f-062)), so the monitoring surface is itself an information-disclosure finding (🟡 [F-061](#f-061)).

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-061](#f-061) — No structured security event logging for authentication or authorization actions.
- 🟡 [F-062](#f-062) — Prometheus metrics endpoint unauthenticated; user and operational counters visible to any caller.

<a id="automated-sca-scanning"></a>
#### 6.11.4 Automated SCA scanning

**Status:** 🟢 Adequate - GitHub Actions CodeQL is wired for JavaScript/TypeScript and runs on every push and pull request; the `.github/workflows/` directory includes dedicated lint and test jobs.

CodeQL is configured in `.github/workflows/` and runs static analysis on the JavaScript/TypeScript codebase. The CI pipeline runs on every push and pull request via `ci.yml`.

**Security assessment**

CodeQL provides genuine static analysis coverage for common vulnerability classes (injection, prototype pollution, XSS sinks). The pipeline also runs lint checks, which catch code-style issues that overlap with security patterns. The limitation is that CodeQL alone does not perform dependency vulnerability scanning (SCA) - `npm audit` is not wired as a CI gate - so known-vulnerable package versions in `package.json` are not blocked at merge time. Adding `npm audit --audit-level=high` as a required check would close the SCA gap without additional tooling.

**Relevant findings**

- No SCA-specific finding is routed here; the gap is covered by 🟠 [F-030](#f-030) (postinstall hook execution) and 🟠 [F-031](#f-031) (mutable action tags).

<a id="automated-dependency-updates"></a>
#### 6.11.5 Automated dependency updates

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no Dependabot, Renovate, or equivalent automated dependency-update workflow is present in the repository.

No `.github/dependabot.yml` or `renovate.json` configuration file is present. Dependency updates are applied manually when maintainers choose to run `npm update` or bump versions.

**Security assessment**

Without automated update tooling, known-vulnerable versions of direct and transitive dependencies accumulate silently. Given that `.npmrc` disables lockfile generation (🟠 [F-032](#f-032)), even the manual update path produces non-deterministic installs. Adding a Dependabot configuration targeting `npm` in the repository root would surface vulnerable packages as pull requests with remediation context.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-032](#f-032) — Lockfile generation disabled; dependency tree is non-deterministic across installs.
- 🟡 [F-059](#f-059) — Several CI workflows use mutable major-version action tags that are not automatically updated when security patches are released.

<a id="lockfile-hygiene"></a>
#### 6.11.6 Lockfile hygiene

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - `.npmrc` sets `package-lock=false`, disabling lockfile generation and causing every `npm install` to re-resolve the full dependency tree from the registry.

`.npmrc:1` contains `package-lock=false`. The Dockerfile installer stage (`npm install --omit=dev`), the six CI test jobs, and the release workflow (`npm install --production`) all run `npm install` without a lockfile to constrain resolution.

**Security assessment**

A `package.json` entry of `"somelib": "^2.3.0"` means every install resolves independently against the registry. A malicious package publish that satisfies the semver range installs on the next `npm install` call without any human review, regardless of whether a reviewer approved the prior passing run. The fix is to re-enable lockfile generation (`npm config delete package-lock` or remove the `.npmrc` line), commit the resulting `package-lock.json`, and configure `npm ci` in all CI jobs to enforce the locked tree.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-032](#f-032) — `.npmrc:1` sets `package-lock=false`, disabling deterministic installs across all environments.

### 6.12 Real-time and Not Applicable Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Missing

**Controls covered:**

- [7.12.1 WebSocket Authorization](#websocket-authorization)
- [7.12.2 Socket.IO Origin Validation](#socketio-origin-validation)

**Implemented controls:** `Socket.IO` v3.1.0 is integrated with the Express server via `lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts`. The client connects to `window.location.origin` from `frontend/src/app/Services/socket-io.service.ts`.

**Assessment:** Socket\.IO is present and in use for real-time challenge notifications. No authentication handshake middleware is wired on the server side, and the CORS origin pin is set to the local development URL rather than the production origin. Challenge flag values are broadcast to every connecting client on connection.

<a id="websocket-authorization"></a>
#### 6.12.1 WebSocket Authorization

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - the `Socket.IO` server accepts connections without authentication; no `io.use(...)` middleware verifies a session token before the connection is established.

`lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts:20` creates the `Socket.IO` server and registers an `io.on('connection', ...)` callback at line 23. The callback fires for any client that completes the WebSocket handshake, regardless of whether they carry a valid session token.

```ts
// registerWebsocketEvents.ts:20–31
io.on('connection', (socket: Socket) => {
  notifications.forEach((notification) =>
    socket.emit('challenge solved', notification)
  )
  // ...
})
```

**Security assessment**

A `grep` for `io.use` across `lib/` returns zero hits - no authentication middleware is registered. Any unauthenticated HTTP client that upgrades the connection to WebSocket receives the current `notifications` array replay, which includes `flag` fields for every challenge solved so far in the session (🟠 [F-048](#f-048)). Adding an `io.use()` middleware that verifies the `Authorization` header or a cookie token before the connection proceeds would close this gap for authenticated challenge events.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-023](#f-023) — No `io.use()` authentication middleware; any client can connect and receive challenge notifications.
- 🟠 [F-048](#f-048) — Challenge flag values broadcast on every new connection via the `notifications` array replay.

<a id="socketio-origin-validation"></a>
#### 6.12.2 `Socket.IO` Origin Validation

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - the CORS origin for the `Socket.IO` handshake is hardcoded to the local development URL (`http://localhost:4200`), which does not match the production deployment and provides no meaningful allowlist in production.

`registerWebsocketEvents.ts:20` initialises the `Socket.IO` server with `origin: 'http://localhost:4200'`. In production, the frontend is served same-origin on port 3000, so this origin string does not match and the configuration provides no real restriction against cross-origin WebSocket connections.

**Security assessment**

The mismatch between the hardcoded development origin and the production origin means either WebSocket connections from legitimate browsers are blocked (if `Socket.IO` enforces the origin strictly), or the origin check is effectively non-functional. In either case, the intent of restricting the WebSocket handshake to the application's own frontend is not achieved. The origin should be configured from an environment variable that matches the actual deployment URL, or derived from the same `cors()` configuration used for the HTTP routes.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟡 [F-056](#f-056) — `Socket.IO` CORS origin hardcoded to `http://localhost:4200`; does not match the production deployment URL.

### 6.13 Defense-in-Depth Summary

**Verdict:** -

The application's strongest positive controls are the distroless runtime image (`gcr.io/distroless/nodejs24-debian13`) and non-root UID (`USER 65532`), which materially limit what a container-escape exploit can do post-entry. The RS256 algorithm for JWT signing, while defeated by the hardcoded key, is the correct algorithm choice and would be sound if the key were rotated and externalized. Angular's default template escaping provides a broad-surface baseline output control, and the CodeQL static analysis in CI catches injection-class issues in new code. The multi-stage Dockerfile prevents build-time tooling from shipping in the runtime image.

The residual architectural risk is concentrated at four control boundaries where a single fix closes multiple findings: (1) externalizing all key material from `lib/insecurity.ts` into runtime-injected environment variables would neutralize JWT forgery, coupon forgery, and security-answer attacks simultaneously; (2) adding algorithm enforcement (`{algorithms:['RS256']}`) to `verify()` and re-deriving role from the validated claim would repair the JWT verification and role-check chain; (3) moving session token storage server-side into an `HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Strict` cookie (Backend-for-Frontend pattern) would eliminate the XSS-token-theft vector and the JavaScript-accessible cookie; (4) switching raw `models.sequelize.query()` calls at `routes/login.ts:34` and the search route to parameterized Sequelize queries would close the SQL injection paths without changing application behavior.
